class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">1.037(9)</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows the XRD patterns of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C, and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite. As we can see, no peaks related to impurities of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C (green line) are detected under the resolution of our X-ray diffractometer, indicating that incorporation of carbon does not affect the cube structure of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. The carbon should be amorphous because no other peaks attributed to crystalline carbon can be detected in the XRD patterns. The residual carbon content is 10.85 wt % in the final products measured using the elemental analyzer. It is obvious that the intensity of diffraction peaks of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C becomes weaker when compared with that of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (red line), which is due to the existence of amorphous carbon impeding the grain growth of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. After a 10-day ball milling (blue line), a much lower intensity and a broader width of the peaks can be observed, especially for the characteristic peaks of (111), (311), (511), and (440). This suggests poor crystallinity of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite with particle size in nanoscales after ball milling. To investigate the structural stability and to confirm the carbon content accurately, TG-DSC analysis of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C are carried out from 25 to 800 °C in the air environment, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b. The slight weight loss of about 1.12% and 1.98% from 25 to 200 °C is due to the evaporation of water (adsorption from the air). However, the change in weight is inconsistent when the temperature is increased beyond 300 °C; consequently, the ex situ XRD tests have also been adopted to confirm the phase transition during the thermal analysis process, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b. As we can see, the weight begins to increase when the temperature is higher than 300 °C, which can be attributed to the formation of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, CoSO<sub>4</sub>, and CoO caused by the decomposition of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and the reaction with oxygen, as shown in the ex situ XRD pattern at 450 °C (point 1). After that, a weight loss happens between 500 and 600 °C, which should be due to the loss of sulfur and the formation of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> detected at 550 °C (point 2). As the temperature continues to increase, the residual CoO transforms into Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, leading to an increase in weight again, detected at 650 °C (point 3). Finally, CoSO<sub>4</sub> decomposes and transforms into Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> completely, and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> becomes the only product at the end, as shown in the ex situ XRD pattern at 800 °C (point 4). Combined with the above analysis, we can conclude that the main weight loss of about 10.37% from 200 to 600 °C for the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C composite is due to the oxidation of the amorphous carbon in air, which is in good agreement with the result of carbon content measured by our elemental analyzer (10.85 wt %).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/medium/la-2016-02870t_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (red line), Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C (green line), and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite (blue line) samples; (b) thermogravimetry differential scanning calorimetry (TG-DSC) curves of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C samples with ex situ XRD tests during the thermal analysis process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> shows the experimental values and fitted curves of XPS for Co 2p and S 2p in Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (a) and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C (b) samples. The oxidation state of S in the two samples can be confirmed to be −2 (2p<sub>3/2</sub>, 162.2 eV), but a mixture of valence states of Co<sup>3+</sup> (2p<sub>3/2</sub>, 786.2 eV), Co<sup>2+</sup> (2p<sub>3/2</sub>, 781.0 eV), and Co<sup>+</sup> (2p<sub>3/2</sub>, 778.5 eV) can be observed, which leads to the average valence state of Co<sup>1.78+</sup> in the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> sample. It is worth noting that the peak intensity for Co 2p and S 2p in Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C is weaker than that of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> because of the existence of carbon layer coating on the surface of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> as described below.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/medium/la-2016-02870t_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. XPS of Co 2p and S 2p in Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (a) and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C (b) samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> shows the SEM images, TEM images, and the EDX analyses of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C, and the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite samples. It is obvious that the dimensions of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles obtained via a solid-state reaction method are in micron size, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, which could lead to the low specific surface area and poor electrochemical performance on account of lengthening the Na-ion diffusion path and decreasing the contact interface between the electrode and the electrolyte. The EDX examination of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> sample confirms the presence of Co and S elements, whereas the bright spots revealed in the mapping show a uniform distribution of Co and S throughout the surface of the sample. A typical lattice-resolved high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image of the powders reveals a single crystalline nature with a <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.29 nm corresponding to the (311) crystal faces. The corresponding selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) dots can be recognized as the cubic symmetry, which is in good agreement with the space structure of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>). When combined with the carbon from sucrose, the particle size becomes smaller and more uniform compared with that of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> sample, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b. However, the most important difference is that an amorphous layer with a thickness of about 5 nm is coated on the crystal face [(440), d-spacing of 0.18 nm] of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, observed using the HRTEM image. This can be considered as a carbon layer formed by the coating on the surface of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles during the annealing process because the EDX examination confirms the presence of carbon in addition to the Co and S elements. It is believed that the amorphous carbon coating on the surface impedes the growth of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> while enhancing the electronic conductivity of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> effectively. After ball milling for 10 days, the particle diameter of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C decreases dramatically to sizes less than 100 nm in average, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c. According to the XRD results in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a, this Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite seems to be almost amorphous. However, some ultrafine nanocrystals coated by the amorphous carbon layer can still be found, and these observations are in good agreement with the SAED image: a halolike ring pattern with some dispersed spots. The elemental mapping analysis shows a highly homogeneous distribution of Co, S, and C in the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite, indicating that Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and C are effectively combined with each other to form an ultrafine nanocomposite during the ball-milling process. As we know, nanosizing the materials can increase the specific surface area greatly, increase the contact area between the electrode and the electrolyte, and decrease the sodium-ion diffusion distances, which will improve the electrochemical performance considerably.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/medium/la-2016-02870t_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. SEM images, TEM images, and EDX analyses of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (a), Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C (b), and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite (c) samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">There is a very small amount of the Fe element mixed in the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C composite after ball milling for 10 days at 500 rpm, confirmed by our XPS result shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/suppl_file/la6b02870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. The content is too small to be detected by the EDX examination. The binding energy of the peak reveals that the oxidation state of the Fe element is +3, which may be due to the combination of iron with oxygen in the steel jar during the ball-milling process under a higher temperature environment caused by the ball milling impact. However, it can be assumed that this trace amount of Fe<sup>3+</sup> would not affect the electrochemical properties as discussed below.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a shows the typical charge/discharge curves for the first three cycles of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C, and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrodes at a current density of 5 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Considering the electrochemical performance of sucrose (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/suppl_file/la6b02870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>) and a low potential that may result in an unnecessary irreversible capacity and excessive volume expansion, we choose 0.5 V as the low cutoff voltage. The first discharge and recharge capacities are ∼318 and ∼191 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>), ∼546 and ∼310 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C), ∼567 and ∼381 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite). The discharge capacities are larger than the recharge capacities for the first cycle, which leads to the relatively poor initial Coulombic efficiencies. This is caused by the excessive fading of capacity between the first sodiation and desodiation processes, which can be attributed to the side reactions such as the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer and the irreversible reaction related to the Na storage mechanism. In addition to the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> electrode, the carbon-coated electrode reactions show high reversibility because the third cycle can repeat well with the specific capacities of the second cycle. From the second cycle, both discharge and charge capacities decrease along with the cycle number and after 30 cycles, the charge capacities of ∼10 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>), ∼185 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C), and ∼320 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite) can be obtained, with the capacity retentions of 5.2%, 59.7%, and 84% relative to the first recharge capacities, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. The two carbon-coated electrodes exhibit a better cycling performance when compared with the pure electrode. The effective combination between Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and the amorphous carbon layer provides not only an integrated continuous electronic conductive network but also good structural flexibility to buffer the volume changes and resist the structure stress during Na<sup>+</sup> sodiation/desodiation. Meanwhile, the carbon layer on the surface suppresses the reaction between the active material and the electrolyte so as to protect the electrode. The reason behind the high specific capacity of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrode compared to Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C electrode can be ascribed to the larger specific surface area due to the nanosize particles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c), thereby increasing the contact area between the electrode and the electrolyte and decreasing the sodium-ion diffusion distances.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/medium/la-2016-02870t_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) First three cycles of galvanostatic discharge/charge curves; (b) cycle performance of the three samples at a current density of 5 mA g<sup>–1</sup> between 0.5 and 2.5 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>; (c) EVS differential capacity data corresponding to (a); and (d) amount of Na<sup>+</sup>/mol insertion in the three electrodes during the first cycle.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The first three consecutive electrochemical voltage spectroscopy (EVS) differential capacity curves are displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c. A small reduction peak (around 1.25 V) in the first cycle is different from the following cycles for the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> electrode. This is a proof for the existence of an irreversible reaction, which could be attributed to the side reaction between the active material and the electrolyte during the first sodiation process. This side reaction destroys the active material and causes the decomposition of the electrolyte, and thus, leads to the poor cycle performance, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The carbon layer on the surface can cut off the contact between Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and the electrolyte and suppress this side reaction (around 1.25 V) effectively. Meanwhile, it contributes to the formation of a possibly more stable SEI layer owing to the preformed SEI effect for polyacrylate coating. The more stable SEI layer can also protect the active material and suppress the electrolyte decomposition especially for the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrode in the initial cycle, thus leading to a significant improvement in the capacity retention and cycle stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The first discharge EVS curve shows a strong reduction peak with a long plateau at 0.7 V, which corresponds to the reaction of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and Na to form Na<sub>2</sub>S and Co, as shown in the ex situ XRD pattern and TEM image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figures 7">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>). In the subsequent cycles, the discharge EVS curves exhibit a similar behavior except the initial discharge process. A higher reduction peak around 1.0 V after the first cycle indicates a change of structure or composition of the electrodes obtained after the first recharge, which are not exactly the same as that of the pristine Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. As it can be seen, the oxidation peaks observed in the EVS curve of the first to third cycles during the charge process remain fairly consistent, indicating a similar desodiation process, whereas the overlapping of the EVS curves in subsequent cycles reveals good reversible electrochemical reactions for the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrode.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d shows the amount of insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>/mol for the three electrodes in the first cycle. The calculation is based on the following equation<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/la-2016-02870t_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span>where <i>C</i> is the specific capacity (mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <i>M</i> is the molar mass (Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, 786.9 g mol<sup>–1</sup>), and <i>x</i> is the electron transfer number of the electrode reaction and also the insertion/deinsertion number of Na<sup>+</sup>. According to the charge and discharge specific capacities in the galvanostatic test (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a), we can calculate the amount of Na<sup>+</sup>/mol insertion and deinsertion during the first cycle using <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/la-2016-02870t_m002.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></span></div><div class="NLM_p">As we can see, a total of 9.33, 16.01, and 16.64 mol Na<sup>+</sup> are inserted in the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C, and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrodes per formula during the first sodiation, whereas 3.71, 6.91, and 5.43 mol Na<sup>+</sup> are residual in the electrodes after the first desodiation. That means a total of 5.62, 9.10, and 11.21 mol Na<sup>+</sup> per formula participate in the reversible reaction that occurs in the first cycle.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a shows the EIS and the equivalent circuit of the three different Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> electrodes, which were measured after running three cycles with an applied voltage of 2.5 V. Each resistance <i>R</i><sub>i</sub>, <i>R</i><sub>i</sub>||constant phase element (CPE<sub>i</sub>), and W components in the equivalent circuit is sectioned as circuit elements (i)–(iii) with W (iv), which are attributed to the regions shown in the Nyquist plots. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> The depressed semicircles are indicative of the inhomogeneous nature of the electrode, and this is accounted for considering CPE<sub>s</sub> instead of pure capacitors in the equivalent circuit. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 40 to 42">(40-42)</a> For the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> electrode, the region (i) represented by <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> is attributed to the resistance of the electrolyte and the cell components. The depressed semicircle observed in the higher frequency range of 65 kHz to 31.4 Hz is attributed to section (ii); at this stage, the total impedance is made up of contributions from the surface film (<i>R</i><sub>sf</sub>||CPE<sub>sf</sub>) and bulk impedance (<i>R</i><sub>b</sub>||CPE<sub>b</sub>), but these could not be separated because of the observation of a single semicircle in the spectra. <i>R</i><sub>sf</sub>||CPE<sub>sf</sub> is ascribed to the resistance offered to Na-ion migration through the surface film (<i>R</i><sub>sf</sub>) and the corresponding capacitance (CPE<sub>sf</sub>). <i>R</i><sub>b</sub>||CPE<sub>b</sub> is the impedance encountered for Na-ion migration through the bulk of the electrode (<i>R</i><sub>b</sub>) with the associated capacitance (CPE<sub>b</sub>). The <i>R</i><sub>b</sub> value is a function of both electronic and ionic conductivities of the composite electrode. The circuit element and the semicircle corresponding to section (iii) in the frequency region of 31.4–1.6 Hz is ascribed to the charge transfer resistance <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and double layer capacitance CPE<sub>ct</sub>. The straight line Warburg region is seen in the impedance spectra at very low frequencies (&lt;1.6 Hz), which corresponds to the sodium-diffusion process within the electrodes. It can be clearly observed that the radius of the semicircles in the frequency regions 65–39.8 Hz and 39.8–1.8 Hz for the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C electrode decreases, which means a lower <i>R</i><sub>sf+b</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> compared to those of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> electrode. More obviously, <i>R</i><sub>sf+b</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> could not even be separated out because of the observation of a single semicircle with the smallest radius among the three electrodes in the spectra (&gt;1.5 Hz) for the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrode. This may indicate the lowest <i>R</i><sub>sf+b</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> similar to the previous reports. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/medium/la-2016-02870t_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) EIS plots with equivalent circuit of the three electrodes after three cycles; (b) family of Nyquist plots (Z′ vs −Z″) of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrode at different voltages vs Na during the first cycle and after three cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b shows the EIS of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrode at the selected open-circuit voltages (OCVs), 0.5 and 2.5 V during the first cycle, and fully charged state after three cycles. The Nyquist plots of the fresh cell (under OCV) comprise a depressed semicircle in the high and intermediate frequency regions and a line in the low-frequency region. When discharged to 0.5 V, the semicircle is no longer depressed (touches the <i>Z</i>′-axis); its diameter reduces enormously, which indicates a significant decrease in the impedance. This significant decrease in impedance can be ascribed to the formation of electronically-conducted metal nanoparticles based on the discussion of ex-situ XRD and TEM in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figures 7">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a> which confirms a conversion  reaction occurred to form nanocrystal Co particles similar to Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> When recharged to 2.5 V in the first cycle, and after three cycles, the formation of the SEI film can be confirmed by the semicircle in the high-frequency region, and a lower charge transfer impedance can be observed in the intermediate frequency region.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/medium/la-2016-02870t_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Ex situ XRD spectra of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrode before cycling (black line), discharged to 0.5 V (red line), recharged to 2.5 V (green line) during the first cycle, and after 30 times of Na-ion insertion/extraction (blue line).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the structure change of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> upon sodiation and desodiation processes, the ex situ XRD measurements on several Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrodes at different states were taken to show the evolution of peaks in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>. Before cycling, the XRD spectrum of the initial electrode (black line) is representative of the stable Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite structure (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a). With the process of sodiation, the characteristic peaks vanish and no obvious diffraction peaks can be detected when the electrode is discharged to 0.5 V (red line), except for the weaker and broader peak corresponding to the (111) crystal surface of nanocrystal nature Co (ICSD card no.622443) formed during the discharge process. Therefore, we can infer that the Na storage mechanism that happens for the electrode is based on conversion reactions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> When recharged to 2.5 V, the crystal structure of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> is irreversible, where no obvious diffraction peaks can be detected and the structure becomes highly disordered and completely amorphous after the first sodiation and desodiation process (green line). Nevertheless, the XRD spectrum of the electrode after 30 cycles (blue line) reveals that this disordered structure still allows for the subsequent reversible cycles demonstrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b.</div><div class="NLM_p">The corresponding ex situ TEM analysis of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrode discharged to 0.5 V and then recharged to 2.5 V is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>. After fully discharged to 0.5 V (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a), the particle size of the active material becomes larger when compared with that of the pristine Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c), which confirms the volume expansion during the sodiation process. However, according to the further HRTEM analysis of the selected region from the sodiation product, well-defined nanocrystals dispersed in an amorphous matrix can be found. These stripes of nanocrystals with a d-spacing of 2.05 nm can be confirmed as metallic Co corresponding to the (111) crystal faces, which coincides with the results of the ex situ XRD pattern (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>). Meanwhile, we can infer that Na<sub>2</sub>S is also the main product dispersed around the nanocrystal Co in the form of amorphous carbon based on the conversion reaction storage mechanism. When recharged to 2.5 V, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b, the particle size recovers to nanometer sizes after the desodiation process, which matches well with the size of the pristine Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c), and the electrode materials have been amorphous, as revealed by HRTEM imaging. This result is fairly consistent with the highly disordered structure of the ex situ XRD result (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>). On the basis of the EDX analysis of the two samples, it is found that the content of sodium decreases after the desodiation process; however, there is still a small amount of sodium present in the electrode, which coincides with the amount of Na<sup>+</sup>/mol insertion for the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite in the first cycle (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). This is an important proof that the reaction is not reversible and has completely occurred in the first sodiation and desodiation process.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/medium/la-2016-02870t_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Ex situ TEM and EDX analyses of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrode material discharged to 0.5 V (a) and then recharged to 2.5 V (b) with a current density of 5 mA g<sup>–1</sup> between 0.5 and 2.5 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup> in the first cycle.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/large/la-2016-02870t_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">On the other hand, it is worth noting that we cannot separate out the reversible formation/dissolution process of the SEI film at fully discharged (0.5 V) and recharged (2.5 V) states in our ex situ TEM test (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>) because of the existence of amorphous carbon coating on the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite surface. However, it is believed that the amorphous carbon layer becomes the main place where the reversible formation/dissolution process of the SEI film occurred. According to <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/suppl_file/la6b02870_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, the discharge capacity (∼35 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) and the charge capacity (∼7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) of the amorphous carbon product heated with sucrose are too small compared with those of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite in the voltage window of 0.5–2.5 V. It means that the capacities contributed by the reversible formation/dissolution process of the SEI film (mainly occurred on the amorphous carbon layer) can be ignored. Therefore, the conversion reaction mechanism is suitable for this kind of material.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Although the irreversible structural transformation of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> upon Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction can be confirmed based on the above structural analysis, the better cycle performance suggests that a reversible electrochemical process exists after the first desodiation, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b. Therefore, according to the amount of Na<sup>+</sup>/mol insertion (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d), the following conversion reaction mechanism is proposed for the system in terms of the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrode<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/la-2016-02870t_m003.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.langmuir.6b02870/asset/images/la-2016-02870t_m004.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></span>where 2<i>y</i> = <i>x</i> + (∼1.245). According to the amount of Na<sup>+</sup>/mol insertion observed, a total of 16.64 mol Na<sup>+</sup> per formula is inserted in the initial discharge process, 16 mol Na<sup>+</sup> is inserted for the conversion reaction (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>) corresponding to the theoretical capacity (545 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), and the extra 0.64 mol Na<sup>+</sup> could be attributed to the formation of the SEI layer and the polymeric gel-type layer on the metal nanoparticles because of the decomposition of the solvent in the electrolyte. When recharged to 2.5 V, the electrode materials become a composite electrode that is made up of an amorphous structure containing Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sup>∼1.245+</sup>S<sub><i>y</i></sub> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b), and this Na–Co–S matrix is responsible for the subsequent discharge/charge cycles described by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="4">4</a>.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">4 Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite with a particle size of less than 100 nm has been successfully synthesized via a solid-state reaction followed by a facile mechanical ball-milling treatment with sucrose as the carbon source. When evaluated as an anode material for SIBs, the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C nanocomposite electrode shows a better electrochemical performance compared to the Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/C electrodes, whether in the discharge-recharge capacity or the cycle stability. The small particle size is beneficial to the quick penetration of the electrolyte and the short distance of lithium ions diffusion, whereas the carbon coating film favors the transport of electrons and accommodates the volume expansion/contraction of the composite during the discharge–charge process. The ex situ XRD and TEM tests demonstrate that Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> undergoes a conversion-type sodium storage mechanism. Although the well-crystallized structure of Co<sub>9</sub>S<sub>8</sub> is irreversible and becomes highly disordered during the first sodiation and desodiation process, a new amorphous phase of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sup>∼1.245+</sup>S<sub><i>y</i></sub> is formed and is responsible for the subsequent reversible discharge/charge cycles with a good cycle stability.</div></div><div B.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rechargeable batteries: Challenges old and new</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Solid State Electrochem.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">16</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">2019</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">2029</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s10008-012-1751-2</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D16%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D2019-2029%26journal%3DJ.%2BSolid%2BState%2BElectrochem.%26author%3DJ.%2BB.%2BGoodenough%26title%3DRechargeable%2Bbatteries%253A%2BChallenges%2Bold%2Band%2Bnew%26doi%3D10.1007%252Fs10008-012-1751-2&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.langmuir.6b02870&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fs10008-012-1751-2&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rechargeable batteries: challenges old and new</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Goodenough, John B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Solid State Electrochemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2019-2029</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JSSEFS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1432-8488</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The challenges for rechargeable batteries are cost, safety, energy, d., life, and rate.  Traditional rechargeable batteries based on aq. electrolytes have good rate capabilities but limited energy d. because the voltage for a long shelf-life is restricted to 1.5 V.  The discovery of fast Na ion cond. in β-alumina in 1967 introduced the novel concept of a solid oxide electrolyte and molten electrodes: the sodium-sulfur battery operates at 350 °C.  Interest in rechargeable batteries with aprotic electrolytes was further stimulated by the first energy crisis in the early 1970s.  Since protons are not mobile in aprotic electrolytes, the Li+ ion was the logical choice for the working ion, and on-going work on reversible Li intercalation into layered sulfides suggested the TiS2//Li cell, which was shown in 1976 to have a voltage of V ≃ 2.2 V and good rate capability.  However, the org. liq. carbonates used as electrolytes are flammable, and dendrites growing across the electrolyte from the lithium anode on repeated charge/discharge cycles short-circuited the cells with disastrous consequences.  Safety concerns caused this effort to be dropped.  However, substitution of the layered oxides LiMO2 for the layered sulfides MS2 and reversible intercalation of Li into graphitic carbon without dendrite formation at slow charging rates gave a safe rechargeable lithium ion battery (LIB) of large-enough energy d. to enable the wireless revolution.  Although carbon-buffered alloys now provide anodes that allow a fast charge and have a higher capacity, nevertheless a passivation layer permeable to Li+ forms on the anode surface, and the Li+ in the passivation layer is taken irreversibly from the cathode on the initial charge.  Since the specific capacity of a cell with an insertion-compd. cathode is limited by the latter, strategies to increase the specific capacity for a LIB powering an elec. vehicle or storing electricity from wind or solar farms include a return to consideration of a solid electrolyte.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201100772"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Barpanda, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nishimura, S.-i.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamada, A.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-voltage pyrophosphate cathodes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. It has encouraged materials' chemists to design and develop a variety of polyanionic compds.  This paper reports and summarizes recent progress in the development of alkali metal pyrophosphate based cathode materials for battery applications.  It points out that the pyrophosphate-based polyanionic-framework materials offer a rich crystal chem., ease of synthesis and robust structure offering good alkali-ion mobility.  Thus, they can form a promising class of cathode materials both for lithium- and sodium-ion secondary batteries. These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Preliminary performance data is presented for a novel sodium-ion cell based on a hard carbon/NaVPO4F chem.  To our knowledge this is the first time that a viable sodium-ion system based on a polyanion cathode material has been demonstrated.  The NaVPO4F was prepd. by a simple incorporation reaction involving vanadium(III) phosphate, VPO4 and NaF.  The hard carbon was from a com. source.  Const. current cycling indicated reversible alkali-ion insertion behavior for the fluorophosphate material, while the generally sym. nature of the charge/discharge curves indicated the energetic reversibility of the insertion system.  The av. discharge voltage for the sodium-ion cells was demonstrated to around 3.7 V, a figure comparable with com. available lithium-ion cells.  The reversible specific capacities for the cathode and anode active materials were detd. to be 82 and 202 mAh/g, resp.  Representative test cells cycled more than 30 times before their discharge capacity had declined to less then 50% of the original.  The relatively high first cycle charge inefficiency is thought to be due to irreversible passivation reactions occurring at the carbon surface.  Significant performance enhancements may be made by optimization of the electrolyte system and improving the active material mass balance.  In summary, low overvoltage, good insertion reversibility, and reasonable capacity retention characterize this fluorophosphate-based sodium-ion cell.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1149/2.jes111637"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xia, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahn, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Accurate description of the mech. behavior of cryst. Na alloys is essential in establishing their electrochem. performance as well as their viability as anodes in Na-ion batteries.  Using first principles simulations, we have investigated the intrinsic elastic properties of cryst. Na-M (M = Sn, Pb, Si and Ge) phases obsd. during Na intercalation.  We have obtained the complete set of concn.-dependent anisotropic elastic consts. as well as the av. macroscopic elastic moduli of polycryst. structures.  We find that sodiation of pure M phases leads to a remarkable elastic softening that results in up to 75% deterioration of the elastic moduli.  Our anal. of the electronic charge distribution demonstrates that the elastic softening during sodiation originates from a transition to weaker ionic interat. bonding. The desodiation potentials of Sn anodes are approx. 0.21 V lower than delithiation potentials.  The low capacity and poor rate capability of C/Sn anode in Na-ion batteries is mainly due to the large Na-ion size, resulting in slow Na-ion diffusion and large vol. change of porous C/Sn composite anode during alloy/dealloy reactions.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The effect of operating temp. on performance and aging of Zebra cells was investigated.  The cells were cycled at 260° and 350° using a modified charging regime to accelerate damage. A mechanism based on the overcharge reaction is proposed for the formation of this phase. The factors responsible for Ni grain growth are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2012.05.020"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, J.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The effects of temperature on the electrochemical performance of sodium-nickel chloride batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lu, Xiaochuan; Li, Guosheng; Kim, Jin Y.; Lemmon, John P.; Sprenkle, Vincent L.; Yang, Zhenguo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-nickel chloride (ZEBRA) batteries are typically operated at relatively high temps. (300°) to achieve adequate electrochem. performance.  In the present study, the effects of operating temp. on the electrochem. performance of planar-type Na/NiCl2 batteries were investigated to evaluate the feasibility of battery operation at low temps. (200°).  The planar-type cell was able to be cycled at C/3 rate at as low as 175° despite higher cell polarization.  Overall, low operating temp. resulted in a considerable improvement in the stability of cell performance.  Cell degrdn. was negligible at 175°, while 55% increase in end-of-charge polarization was obsd. at 280° after 60 cycles.  SEM anal. indicated that the degrdn. at higher temps. was related to the particle growth of both nickel and sodium chloride.  The cells tested at lower temps. (200°), however, exhibited a sharp drop in voltage at the end of discharge due to the diffusion limitation, possibly caused by the limited ionic cond. of catholyte or the poor wettability of sodium on the β''-Al2O3 solid electrolyte (BASE).  Therefore, improvements in the ionic cond. of catholyte and sodium wetting as well as redn. in the ohmic resistance of BASE are required to enhance the battery performance at low temps.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1149/1.3428667"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tevar, A. Mn- and Fe-based cathode materials, such as LiMn2O4 and LiFePO4, are used in large-scale batteries for elec. vehicles.  Mn and Fe are abundant elements in the Earth's crust, but Li is not.  In contrast to Li, Na is an attractive charge carrier from elemental abundance.  Recently, some layered materials, where Na can be electrochem. and reversibly extd./inserted, have been reported. Herein, the authors report a new electrode material, P2-Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2, that delivers 190 mA-h/g of reversible capacity in Na cells with the electrochem. active Fe3+/Fe4+ redox.                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhanced Sodium-Ion Battery Performance by Structural Phase Transition from Two-Dimensional Hexagonal-SnS2 to Orthorhombic-SnS</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhou, Tengfei; Pang, Wei Kong; Zhang, Chaofeng; Yang, Jianping; Chen, Zhixin; Liu, Hua Kun; Guo, Zaiping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Structural phase transitions can be used to alter the properties of a material without adding any addnl. elements and are therefore of significant technol. value.  It was found that the hexagonal-SnS2 phase can be transformed into the orthorhombic-SnS phase after an annealing step in an argon atm., and the thus transformed SnS shows enhanced sodium-ion storage performance over that of the SnS2, which is attributed to its structural advantages.  Here, we provide the first report on a SnS@graphene architecture for application as a sodium-ion battery anode, which is built from two-dimensional SnS and graphene nanosheets as complementary building blocks.  The as-prepd. SnS@graphene hybrid nanostructured composite delivers an excellent specific capacity of 940 mAh/g and impressive rate capability of 492 and 308 mAh/g after 250 cycles at the current densities of 810 and 7290 mA/g, resp.  The performance was found to be much better than those of most reported anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  On the basis of combined ex situ Fourier transform IR spectroscopy, XPS, and ex situ X-ray diffraction, the formation mechanism of SnS@graphene and the synergistic Na-storage reactions of SnS in the anode are discussed in detail.  The SnS experienced a two-structural-phase transformation mechanism (orthorhombic-SnS to cubic-Sn to orthorhombic-Na3.75Sn), while the SnS2 experienced a three-structural-phase transformation mechanism (hexagonal-SnS2 to tetragonal-Sn to orthorhombic-Na3.75Sn) during the sodiation process.  The lesser structural changes of SnS during the conversion are expected to lead to good structural stability and excellent cycling stability in its sodium-ion battery performance.  These results demonstrate that the SnS@graphene architecture offers unique characteristics suitable for high-performance energy storage application.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref27" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1002/asia.201400018"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xie, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Su, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dou, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">SnS<sub>2</sub> nanoplatelet@ graphene nanocomposites as high-capacity anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.—Asian J.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">9</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1611</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1617</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Sn-SnS-C nanocomposite as anode host materials for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Lin; Hu, Xiaohong; Qian, Jiangfeng; Pei, Feng; Wu, Fayuan; Mao, Rongjun; Ai, Xinping; Yang, Hanxi; Cao, Yuliang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A Sn-SnS-C nanocomposite is prepd. by simply mech. ball-milling Sn, SnS and C powders.  In this composite, Sn nanocrystals are surface-coated with SnS nanoparticles and uniformly dispersed in the carbon matrix.  During discharge, the SnS particles undergo an electrochem. conversion reaction to generate Sn and Na2S nanocrystals and then the Sn particles alloy with Na to produce the NaSn alloy.  The conversion reaction of SnS and the alloying reaction of Sn with Na are completely reversible, producing a very high reversible Na-storage capacity of &gt;600 mA-h/g at an appropriate low potential of ∼0.7 V.  Since the SnS phase provides an effective buffering matrix to alleviate the volumetric change of the Sn particles during Na insertion and extn., and also serves as a separator to prevent the aggregation of the Sn nanoparticles, the Sn-SnS-C composite anode demonstrates a very good cycling stability with 87% capacity retention over 150 cycles, possibly usable for Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.03.011"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shadike, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, Y.-N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ding, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nam, K.-W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.-Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fu, Z.-W.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The new electrochemical reaction mechanism of Na/FeS<sub>2</sub> cell at ambient temperature</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are promising elec. energy storage systems for sustainable energy storage applications due to the abundance of Na and their low cost. The RGO anode allowed significant Na ion insertion, leading to higher capacity at high c.d. compared to the previously reported results for C materials.                         cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Phosphorus electrodes in sodium cells: small volume expansion by sodiation and the surface-stabilization mechanism in aprotic solvent</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Matsuura, Yuta; Ishikawa, Toru; Kuze, Satoru; Son, Jin-Young; Cui, Yi-Tao; Oji, Hiroshi; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemElectroChem</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrode performance of amorphous phosphorus in aprotic Na cells is examd.  Amorphous phosphorus is electrochem. reduced in the Na cells with a three-electron redox process, crystg. into Na3P.  Na-P bonds in Na3P have high covalent characteristics.  Therefore, the molar volume of Na in Na3P is anomalously small in comparison to other Na-metal alloys that have been used as neg. electrode materials.  The theor. volumetric capacity, calcd. at full vol. expansion of amorphous phosphorus electrodes through sodiation, is expected to be 27% larger than that of metallic sodium.  However, exptl., it is found that severe electrolyte decompn. results in insufficient reversibility of the electrode materials, owing to the highly reactive Na3P surface.  Electrode reversibility is successfully improved by utilizing an electrolyte additive, fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC).  The surface chem. of phosphorus in the aprotic solvent with sodium salts is examd. by hard/soft XPS (PES).  PES studies reveal that FEC effectively stabilizes the solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI), contg. monovalent phosphorus species and sodium fluoride, and thus electrolyte decompn. is partly suppressed by the relatively stable SEI formed on the surface of phosphorus particles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref33" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201305638"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kim, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Choi, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Woo, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mok, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Choi, N.-S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jung, Y.                         cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In Situ Transmission Electron Microscopy Observation of Electrochemical Sodiation of Individual Co9S8-Filled Carbon Nanotubes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Su, Qingmei; Du, Gaohui; Zhang, Jun; Zhong, Yijun; Xu, Bingshe; Yang, Yuehai; Neupane, Suman; Li, Wenzhi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3620-3627</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The comprehension of fundamental electrochem. behavior and sodiation mechanism is crit. for the design of high-performance electrode materials for sodium-ion (Na-ion) batteries.  The electrochem. sodiation process and microstructure evolution of individual Co9S8-filled carbon nanotubes (CNTs) were directly visualized and studied using in situ TEM.  Upon the 1st sodiation, a reaction front propagates progressively along the filling nanowire, causing the filled CNT to inflate.  The filled CNTs behave differently depending on their structures and the magnitude of the sodiation voltage.  For a Co9S8-filled CNT with an open end, the sodiated Co9S8 filler shows a substantial axial elongation of 120.8% and a small radial swelling due to the extrusion of CNT walls.  But the closed CNT shows a major radial expansion of 40.6% and a small axial elongation because of the mech. confinement of the carbon shells.  After sodiation, the spacing between the carbon shells increases from 3.4 to 3.8 Å due to the Na+-ion insertion and the single-cryst. Co9S8 filler converts to numerous Co nanograins dispersed in a Na2S matrix.  Compared with the gentle microstructure evolution of the CNT under small charging voltage, a strong electrochem. reaction accompanying drastic swelling and fracturing of CNT shells is obsd. for the CNT electrode under large charging voltage.  The observations provide direct evidence and important insights for the electrochem. process of CNT-based composite materials in Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref36" class="refNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit36"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Larson, A. More significantly, we have found that Li2MoO4 can be used as an anode material for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs). M.; Rao, G. V. Subba; Chowdari, B. V. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2004</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9-10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1565-1576</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Science B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique (GITT) at room temp. (25 °C) and at 50 °C and electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) at 25 °C as a function of cell voltage and cycle no. were performed on the layered cathode, Li(Ni1/2Mn1/2)O2 and the results were correlated with its known electrochem. performance.  Comparison of the voltage vs. capacity profiles and the cycling performance reveals that a higher extn. and reversible capacity can be obtained by means of increasing the upper cut-off voltage or by changing the cycling mode (from const.-current (CC) to const.-current-const.-voltage (CCCV)) at 25 °C or by increasing the temp. to 50 °C for V &gt;4.2 V vs. Li metal, but this leads to capacity-fading over 50 cycles.  A min. in the Li-ion diffusion coeff. (DLi) vs. voltage curve was obsd. at ∼3.8 V coinciding with the plateau region in the charge-discharge profiles.  The DLi is almost unchanged with a value of 3.0(±0.5)×10-10 cm2/s in the voltage range, 3.8-4.3 V vs. Li.  EIS studies as a function of cycle no. after charging the cells to ∼3.8 and 4.4 V, after they were cycled in the CC and CCCV modes show that while the surface film and the bulk contribution of the cell resistances remain small and unchanged, a significant increase in the charge transfer resistance (Rct) occurs, revealing a pronounced electrode polarization.  This polarization may be the reason for the obsd. capacity-fading in Li(Ni1/2Mn1/2)O2 when charged to voltages &gt;4.2 V.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref42" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1149/1.1521754"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Shaju, K. R.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical kinetic studies of Li-ion in O<sub>2</sub>-structured Li<sub>2/3</sub>(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> and Li<sub>(2/3)+<i>x</i></sub>(Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>)O<sub>2</sub> by EIS and GITT</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. P.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Identifying the local structures formed during lithiation of the conversion material, iron fluoride, in a Li ion battery: A solid-state NMR, X-ray diffraction, and pair distribution function analysis study</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Am. Chem. Soc.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2009</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">131</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">10525</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">10536</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/ja902639w</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D131%26publication_year%3D2009%26pages%3D10525-10536%26journal%3DJ.%2BAm.%2BChem.%2BSoc.%26author%3DN.%2BYamakawa%26author%3DM.%2BJiang%26author%3DB.%2BKey%26author%3DC.%2BP.%2BGrey%26title%3DIdentifying%2Bthe%2Blocal%2Bstructures%2Bformed%2Bduring%2Blithiation%2Bof%2Bthe%2Bconversion%2Bmaterial%252C%2Biron%2Bfluoride%252C%2Bin%2Ba%2BLi%2Bion%2Bbattery%253A%2BA%2Bsolid-state%2BNMR%252C%2BX-ray%2Bdiffraction%252C%2Band%2Bpair%2Bdistribution%2Bfunction%2Banalysis%2Bstudy%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fja902639w&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.langmuir.6b02870&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fja902639w&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Identifying the Local Structures Formed during Lithiation of the Conversion Material, Iron Fluoride, in a Li Ion Battery: A Solid-State NMR, X-ray Diffraction, and Pair Distribution Function Analysis Study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yamakawa, Naoko; Jiang, Meng; Key, Baris; Grey, Clare P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">131</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">30</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10525-10536</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The structural transformations that occur when FeF3 is cycled at room temp. in a Li cell were investigated using a combination of x-ray diffraction (XRD), pair distribution function (PDF) anal., and magic-angle-spinning NMR spectroscopy.  Two regions are seen on discharge.  The first occurs between Li = 0 and 1.0 and involves an insertion reaction.  This first region actually comprises two steps.  First, a two-phase reaction between Li = 0 and 0.5 occurs, and the Li0.5FeF3 phase that is formed gives rise to a Li NMR resonance due to Li+ ions near both Fe3+ and Fe2+ ions.  On the basis of the PDF data, the local structure of this phase is closer to the rutile structure than the original ReO3 structure.  Second, a single-phase intercalation reaction occurs between Li = 0.5 and 1.0, for which the Li NMR data indicate a progressive increase in the concn. of Fe2+ ions.  In the second region, the conversion reaction, superparamagnetic, nanosized (∼3 nm) Fe metal is formed, as indicated by the XRD and NMR data, along with some LiF and a third phase that is rich in Li and F.  The charge process involves the formation of a series of intercalation phases with increasing Fe oxidn. state, which, on the basis of the Li NMR and PDF data, have local structures that are similar to the intercalation phases seen during the first stage of the discharge process.  The solid-state NMR and XRD results for the rutile phase FeF2 are presented for comparison, and the data indicate that an insertion reaction also occurs, which is accompanied by the formation of LiF.  This is followed by the formation of Fe nanoparticles and LiF via a conversion reaction.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187e3b3b48075b-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1021/cr100290v"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kintner-Meyer, M.                         rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rechargeable batteries: challenges old and new</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Goodenough, John B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Solid State Electrochemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2019-2029</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JSSEFS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1432-8488</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The challenges for rechargeable batteries are cost, safety, energy, d., life, and rate.  Traditional rechargeable batteries based on aq. electrolytes have good rate capabilities but limited energy d. because the voltage for a long shelf-life is restricted to 1.5 V.  The discovery of fast Na ion cond. in β-alumina in 1967 introduced the novel concept of a solid oxide electrolyte and molten electrodes: the sodium-sulfur battery operates at 350 °C.  Interest in rechargeable batteries with aprotic electrolytes was further stimulated by the first energy crisis in the early 1970s.  Since protons are not mobile in aprotic electrolytes, the Li+ ion was the logical choice for the working ion, and on-going work on reversible Li intercalation into layered sulfides suggested the TiS2//Li cell, which was shown in 1976 to have a voltage of V ≃ 2.2 V and good rate capability.  However, the org. liq. carbonates used as electrolytes are flammable, and dendrites growing across the electrolyte from the lithium anode on repeated charge/discharge cycles short-circuited the cells with disastrous consequences.  Safety concerns caused this effort to be dropped.  However, substitution of the layered oxides LiMO2 for the layered sulfides MS2 and reversible intercalation of Li into graphitic carbon without dendrite formation at slow charging rates gave a safe rechargeable lithium ion battery (LIB) of large-enough energy d. to enable the wireless revolution.  Although carbon-buffered alloys now provide anodes that allow a fast charge and have a higher capacity, nevertheless a passivation layer permeable to Li+ forms on the anode surface, and the Li+ in the passivation layer is taken irreversibly from the cathode on the initial charge.  Since the specific capacity of a cell with an insertion-compd. cathode is limited by the latter, strategies to increase the specific capacity for a LIB powering an elec. vehicle or storing electricity from wind or solar farms include a return to consideration of a solid electrolyte.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201100772"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Barpanda, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nishimura, S.-i.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamada, A.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-voltage pyrophosphate cathodes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.
